Significantly differential genes that are over twofold up- or down-regulated are indicated by an asterisk and bold text (&lt;em&gt;P&lt;/em&gt; &lt; 0.01).&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 6." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/21/E4296/F6.medium.gif" width="420" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 6." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/21/E4296/F6.medium.gif" width="420" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/21/E4296/F6.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 6." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/21/E4296/F6.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/17348" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 6.</span> <p id="p-42" class="first-child"><em>C. zofingiensis</em> RNA-Seq expression of select genes during the transition to high light. RNA-Seq was performed on cultures following a shift from medium light (ML, 100 μmol photons⋅m<sup>−2</sup>⋅s<sup>−1</sup>) to high light (HL, 400–450 μmol photons⋅m<sup>−2</sup>⋅s<sup>−1</sup>) as described in <a id="xref-fig-5-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>. <em>C. zofingiensis</em> genes potentially involved in carotenoid biosynthesis, nonphotochemical quenching (NPQ), and chlorophyll biosynthesis and degradation were identified by manual curation. (<em>Left</em>) The regularized log<sub>2</sub>-transformed fold change between HL and ML for each of these genes at each time point is plotted as a heatmap. (<em>Right</em>) The regularized log<sub>2</sub>-transformed fold change between each time point relative to <em>t</em> = 0 is plotted. Significantly differential genes that are over twofold up- or down-regulated are indicated by an asterisk and bold text (<em>P</em> &lt; 0.01).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-43">The high-quality genome and transcriptome we generated in combination with the high light RNA-Seq experiment allowed us to identify candidates for additional genes involved in astaxanthin biosynthesis and accumulation. As mentioned above, little is known about the mechanism of translocation of the astaxanthin precursor(s) out of the chloroplast, the hydroxylation of the astaxanthin precursor, transport of astaxanthin into lipid droplets, or the esterification of astaxanthin. We identified genes putatively involved in astaxanthin biosynthesis by examining significantly differentially expressed genes with high increases in gene expression during the shift to high light, and looking for annotated activity compatible with hypothetical mechanisms of astaxanthin biosynthesis. Genes that are up-regulated early during high light that may be implicated in the astaxanthin pathway include four ABC transporters (Cz04g21110, Cz05g17060, Cz09g27180, and Cz08g16130), two cytochrome P450 proteins (Cz10g28330 and Cz11g14160), and an acyltransferase (Cz02g29020). The ABC transporters may form a complex that exports the astaxanthin precursor(s) from the chloroplast. The cytochrome P450 proteins could be involved in hydroxylation of astaxanthin precursors in the cytosol, and the acyltransferase could be involved in esterification of astaxanthin. However, these genes need to be experimentally tested in vitro and/or in vivo to determine whether they function in the pathway. Improving understanding of the astaxanthin pathway may provide opportunities to enhance astaxanthin production in this species and others.</p><p id="p-44">In addition to changes in carotenoid biosynthesis, we also investigated other algal high light responses, including photoprotective mechanisms and chlorophyll metabolism. In photosynthetic organisms, excess light must be safely dissipated to prevent oxidative damage. <em>C. reinhardtii</em> transiently expresses PSBS at the onset of high light and LHCSR proteins accumulate under high light, and this accumulation is correlated with nonphotochemical quenching capacity (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>, <a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>). Whereas <em>C. reinhardtii</em> has multiple copies of both <em>LHCSR</em> and <em>PSBS</em> (<a id="xref-ref-51-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>, <a id="xref-ref-52-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>), we found only single copies of <em>LHCSR</em> and <em>PSBS</em> in <em>C. zofingiensis</em>, despite having high nonphotochemical quenching capacity (<a id="xref-ref-9-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>). As expected, both <em>LHCSR</em> and <em>PSBS</em> were up-regulated at the early time points during the shift to high light and, in particular, at 1 h, which is consistent with observations of <em>C. reinhardtii</em> during the dark-to-light transition (<a id="xref-ref-45-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">45</a>). Similar to the carotenoid biosynthesis genes, under the diurnal cycle <em>LHCSR</em> and <em>PSBS</em> are down-regulated by the end of the day (6 and 12 h) in both conditions (<a id="xref-fig-6-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>). Reduction in chlorophyll is another common physiological response of algae exposed to high light (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>). Accordingly, during the shift to high light, many <em>C. zofingiensis</em> genes involved in chlorophyll synthesis were down-regulated and chlorophyll degradation genes were up-regulated (<a id="xref-fig-6-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>). The combination of these changes would lead to a reduction in chlorophyll content either during acclimation or as a stress response to high light.</p></div><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>Annotation of Metabolic Pathways and Photosynthesis-Related Genes.</h3><p id="p-45">Genes encoding homologs of the primary metabolic pathway enzymes involved with carbon, carotenoids, chlorophyll, fatty acids, lipids, and proteins involved in the composition, assembly, and regulation of the photosynthetic apparatus, were preliminarily identified by using the BLAT sequence search tool (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>) against a <em>C. zofingiensis</em> draft genome (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1619928114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1619928114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S3</a>). Based on the quality of alignments and comparison with well-characterized, closely-related plant and algal query sequences, the targeted gene models in <em>C. zofingiensis</em> were submitted as queries in reciprocal BLAST searches against the NCBI RefSeq nonredundant protein database to confirm coverage, domain architecture, and similarity across closely-related homologs. Because of the high quality of the <em>C. zofingiensis</em> assembly, this procedure resulted in a nearly complete list of putative genes needed to complete each pathway. Identified gene models were used to assess the quality of the automated gene family analysis across the six species of <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1619928114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1619928114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S1</a>, and the automated analysis was used to confirm additional candidate models and expand the set of annotations. Based on high sequence similarities and conservation of functional domains, we are generally confident in assignments of homology. However, it is possible that additional functional isoforms composed of more divergent sequences may also be present, having been missed by the parameters used for BLAT, BLAST, and the automated gene family analysis.</p><p id="p-46">Identification and annotation of genes involved in lipid biosynthesis can provide targets for exploitation of <em>C. zofingiensis</em> for biofuel production. Using other oleaginous organisms as a guide, we would expect a robust oil-producing microalga to have an expanded family of acyltransferases. The <em>C. zofingiensis</em> diacylglycerol acyltransferases (DGAT) are too divergent from the protein sequences of type 1 DGAT and DGTT (type 2 DGATs; ref. <a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>) in <em>C. reinhardtii</em>, <em>A. thaliana</em>, and <em>M. neglectum</em> to identify the corresponding genes via BLAT. Using a more sensitive BLAST search with both types of DGAT sequences from <em>C. subellipsoidea</em> C<em>-</em>169 (gi|545360296), <em>Chlorella vulgaris</em> (gb|ALP13863.1), <em>Nannochloropsis gaditana</em> (gb|EWM23187.1), <em>A. thaliana</em> (gi|15224779, gi|18409359), and <em>C. reinhardtii</em> (Cre01.g045903, Cre03.g205050), additional copies of DGAT type 1- and DGTT-encoding genes were identified in <em>C. zofingiensis,</em> and yet more were identified by using the automated gene family analysis. In total, 11 genes were identified that have either an LPLAT (lysophospholipid acyltransferase) domain or a closely-related MBOAT (membrane bound O-acyltransferase) domain (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1619928114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1619928114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S3</a>). We have tentatively assigned these genes as encoding proteins with diacylglycerol acyltransferase activity; however, some of these <em>C. zofingiensis</em> gene models have higher similarity to predicted proteins of unknown function than to annotated type 1 or type 2 DGAT proteins from other closely related organisms. Our finding of multiple copies of putative <em>DGAT</em> and <em>DGTT</em> genes in <em>C. zofingiensis</em> is consistent with transcriptome results from the closely-related ATCC 30412 strain (<a id="xref-ref-40-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">40</a>). It is also possible, although unlikely, that one or multiple additional copies of <em>DGAT</em> or <em>DGTT</em> may be yet unidentified because of a gene modeling or assembly problem.</p><p id="p-47">Homologous gene models were also identified for components of the photosynthetic apparatus and its assembly, including proteins that compose PSI, PSII, the major and minor light harvesting antennae, the cytochrome <em>b</em><sub>6</sub><em>f</em> complex, the chloroplast ATP synthase complex, soluble electron carriers, and known assembly factors for these complexes (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1619928114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1619928114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S3</a>). Thirteen of the <em>C. zofingiensis</em> light-harvesting complex (<em>LHC</em>) genes are predicted to be more like PSI-associated LHC genes (<em>LHCA</em>s) while nine are predicted to be PSII-associated LHC genes (<em>LHCB</em>s) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1619928114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1619928114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S3</a>). This distribution is in contrast to that found in <em>C. reinhardtii</em>, which has nine of each of <em>LHCA</em> and <em>LHCB</em>. Further experimental work is needed to confirm the expression profiles and photosystem association of each of these putative LHC proteins, especially under different light and stress conditions. Of note is one LHC model (Lhcb-like3, Cz04g24050) with little to no transcriptional expression detected in any of our RNA-Seq conditions.</p></div></div><div class="section conclusions" id="sec-8"><h2 class="">Conclusions</h2><p id="p-48" class="flushleft">Our analyses of the <em>C. zofingiensis</em> genome, transcriptome, astaxanthin-deficient mutants, and RNA expression changes under high light reveal insights into the basic biology of the green lineage of photosynthetic organisms and the carotenoid biosynthesis pathway. We present, in the tradition of model organisms, a high-quality chromosome-level assembly with independent genome validation. The compact ∼58-Mbp genome has balanced G+C content and is rich in protein-coding sequence with few long exons per gene and relatively little repetitive sequence. We identified ortholog families for the majority of <em>C. zofingiensis</em> genes. The gene density is uniform over chromosomes and a syntenic comparison with other algae uncovered highly significant genomically localized blocks of genes in putative orthologous relationships; however, gene order and strands within blocks are scrambled. We have shown that <em>BKT1</em> is critical for the production of astaxanthin and have identified candidate genes that could be missing components in astaxanthin biosynthesis and accumulation pathways. The addition of genomics to the experimental toolkit for <em>C. zofingiensis</em> makes it an attractive alga not only for fundamental studies of its biology but also the economically viable and environmentally sustainable production of biofuels and important bioproducts.</p></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-49">The cryo-soft X-ray tomography was supported by the US Department of Energy, Office of Science, Basic Energy Sciences, Chemical Sciences, Geosciences, and Biosciences Division under field work proposal SISGRKN.
